Acts That Revoke a Will: Ownership Removal and Customary Indicators
List and explain acts indicating or negating revocation of a will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts constitute or negate revocation of a will according to the Hanafi school and apply the underlying principle of ownership removal versus subordinate acts.

Core Idea
An act that removes ownership or shows personal use revokes a will; subordinate or customary acts do not.

When a testator makes a will, his subsequent actions may indicate a change of mind. The Hanafi school derives specific legal indicators of revocation (ruju') from custom (urf) and the principle that a will is only effective in the testator's ownership. This passage lists and explains which acts constitute revocation and which do not, providing the rationale for each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key principle governing revocation
The passage states that any act removing the testator's ownership from the bequeathed item (musa bih) constitutes revocation. This is because a will only takes effect in the testator's property.
This principle is derived from the nature of a will as a transfer of ownership at death.
2
Analyze acts that remove ownership
Sale, gift, and slaughter are given as examples. Even if the testator repurchases or revokes the gift, the initial act still counts as revocation.
The significance is the intent to alienate the property at that moment.
3
Distinguish acts that are subordinate or customary
Whitewashing or demolishing a house does not remove ownership; the original remains. Washing a garment is not revocation because customarily one washes a garment before giving it as a gift.
These acts are seen as confirmations (taqrir) of the will, not contradictions.
4
Apply the rule of inseparable additions
If the testator adds something inseparable to the bequeathed item, that also indicates revocation because the addition becomes part of his property and cannot be separated without damaging the original.
This extends the principle to cases where the testator enhances the property without removing ownership.

And the removal of the addition is not impossible, because the addition is acquired from the testator into his ownership, unlike whitewashing the house that is bequeathed and demolishing its structure, because that is a subordinate act. Explanation: If the testator has added something to the bequeathed item that cannot be separated without damage, that act is also evidence that the testator has revoked. The author has given many examples in the book. However, if the testator had the bequeathed house whitewashed or had it demolished, these two acts are not evidence of revocation, because the original remains and these things are subordinate, and the testator's act is not on the original but on the subordinate. Every act that causes the removal of the testator's ownership is evidence of revocation. And every act that necessitates the removal of the testator's ownership is revocation, such as when he sells the bequeathed item, then buys it back, or gifts it, then revokes the gift, because the will is only effective in his ownership. So when he removes it, it is revocation. And slaughtering the bequeathed sheep is revocation because it is customarily for his need, so this meaning also becomes an original. And washing the bequeathed garment is not revocation, because whoever intends to give his garment to someone else would usually wash it, so it is a confirmation. Translation: And every act that causes the removal of the testator's ownership is revocation, as when he sells the bequeathed item, then buys it, or gifts it, then revokes the gift, because the will is only effective in his ownership. So when he removes it, it becomes revocation. And slaughtering the bequeathed sheep is revocation, because customarily that is turning it to his need. But washing the bequeathed garment is not revocation, because whoever intends to give his garment to another would customarily wash it, so it is a confirmation of the will. Explanation: And if the testator performs any act that is a cause of removing his ownership, that too is evidence of revocation. For example, if the testator sells the bequeathed item, even if he later buys it back, this sale is evidence of revocation; or if he gifts it, even if he later revokes the gift, the gifting is evidence of revocation, because the will is only effective in his ownership, and when he removes his ownership through these acts, it is evidence of revocation. And if the testator slaughters the bequeathed sheep, that is also evidence of revocation, because the custom is that the slaughterer slaughters for himself. So this becomes a general rule: when the testator performs an act on the bequeathed item for his own need, it is evidence of revocation. And if the bequeathed item is a garment and the testator washes it, this washing is not revocation, because it is customary that one who gives his garment to another gives it washed. Therefore, it is not counted as evidence of revocation; rather, it is confirmation and affirmation of the will.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, listing acts that indicate a testator's revocation (ruju') of his will and acts that do not. The author, following the Hanafi school, explains that any act removing the testator's ownership from the bequeathed item (musa bih) constitutes revocation. Examples include selling the item (even if later repurchased) or gifting it (even if later revoked). The proof is that a will is only valid in the testator's ownership, so removing that ownership nullifies the will. Similarly, slaughtering a bequeathed sheep indicates revocation because by custom it is used for personal need. However, acts that do not remove ownership and are subordinate to the original, such as whitewashing or demolishing a house, do not count as revocation. Also, washing a bequeathed garment is not revocation because it is customary to give clothes washed, so it confirms the will. The passage also covers the case where the testator adds something inseparable to the bequeathed item—this is also evidence of revocation because the addition becomes part of his property. The key principle is that the testator's intent to revoke is inferred from actions that either transfer ownership or use the item for personal benefit.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a will; the testator's act or word that nullifies the bequest.
موصیٰ بہ (musa bih): The item or property that is the subject of the will.
وصیت (wasiyyah): A bequest or will, a legal instrument to transfer property after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts is considered a revocation of a will according to the Hanafi school?
A) Washing the bequeathed garment
B) Slaughtering the bequeathed sheep
C) Whitewashing the bequeathed house
D) Adding a room to the bequeathed house

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a will is revoked by any act that removes the testator's ownership or indicates personal use; subordinate or customary acts do not affect the will.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does Denial of a Will Revoke It?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on denial as revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on whether denial of a will constitutes revocation, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A testator's denial of his will does not revoke it according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf. The issue: if a testator (musi) denies ever making a will, does that denial (juhud) legally count as a revocation (ruju')? The practical consequence is significant: if denial is revocation, the will becomes null; if not, the will remains valid. We will explore the proofs of each imam and see how later Hanafi authorities resolved the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act and the two positions
State the act: the testator's verbal denial that he ever made a will. Identify the two Hanafi positions: Imam Muhammad holds it is not revocation; Imam Abu Yusuf holds it is revocation.
This dispute is recorded in al-Hidayah and other major Hanafi texts.
2
Understand Imam Abu Yusuf's proof
Imam Abu Yusuf argues that revocation is negation of the will in the present only, while denial negates in both past and present. Since the present negation alone suffices for revocation, the stronger negation (including past) should be even more deserving of being considered revocation.
This is an a fortiori (awla) argument.
3
Understand Imam Muhammad's proof
Imam Muhammad gives two proofs: (1) Denial negates the past, so present non-existence is automatic; if the will existed, the denial is meaningless. (2) Revocation affirms past existence and negates present, while denial negates both, so it is not revocation in reality. He analogizes: denial of marriage is not divorce.
The analogy strengthens the argument that denial is not the same as revocation.
4
Examine the reconciliation and tarjih
Some scholars reconcile conflicting reports from Imam Muhammad by context: denial in the presence/absence of the legatee. But al-Marghinani (al-Hidayah) attributes the 'denial not revocation' view to Muhammad and the opposite to Abu Yusuf, and this is the preferred interpretation.
The tarjih (preference) is for Imam Muhammad's view, making it the mufta bihi.
5
State the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that denial does not constitute revocation. Therefore, if a testator denies his will, the bequest remains valid and must be executed unless there is independent evidence of revocation.
This is the standard fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

If the testator denies the will, should it be considered revocation of the will or not? Statements of the jurists: He (al-Quduri) said: Whoever denies the will, it is not revocation. Thus Muhammad mentioned. And Abu Yusuf said: It is revocation, because revocation is negation in the present, and denial is negation in the past and present, so it is all the more likely to be revocation. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And whoever denies the will, this denial is not revocation. Muhammad mentioned likewise. And Abu Yusuf said: It will be revocation, because revocation is negation in the present, and denial is negation in the past and present, so it is all the more likely that it is itself revocation. Explanation: It has passed earlier that revocation of a will is permissible, and some acts were mentioned that are evidence of revocation. Now the issue being explained here is that if the testator denies the will itself, what is the ruling? The author presents two statements: one of Imam Muhammad and the other of Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad mentioned that this denial is not revocation, and Imam Abu Yusuf said that it is revocation. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that the reality of revocation is that it affirms the will in the past and negates it in the present, and revocation is permissible. The meaning of denial of the will is that it is a denial in the past and in the present. So when negation in the present alone constitutes revocation, then negation in both past and present is all the more likely to be revocation. Note: Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Kabir that denial is not revocation, and in al-Mabsut it is mentioned that denial is revocation. Some jurists said that what is mentioned in al-Mabsut is carried on the case when the revocation (denial) is in the presence of the legatee (musa lahu), and what is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir is carried on the case when the revocation is in the absence of the legatee. And some said that what is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir is the statement of Imam Muhammad, and what is mentioned in al-Mabsut is the statement of Imam Abu Yusuf. The author of al-Hidayah also holds this opinion, and this is correct. (Zayla'i, vol. 6, p. 718). And for Muhammad, two proofs: And for Muhammad: denial is negation in the past, and non-existence in the present is necessary from that. And if it is established in the present, the denial becomes meaningless. Or because revocation is affirmation in the past and negation in the present, while denial is negation in the past and present, so it is not revocation in reality. For this reason, denial of marriage is not divorce. Translation: And Muhammad's proof is that denial negates in the past, and non-existence in the present is a necessity of that. And when falsity in the present is established, the denial becomes meaningless. And because revocation affirms in the past and negates in the present, while denial negates in the past and present, so it is not truly revocation. For this reason, denial of marriage is not divorce. Explanation: This is Imam Muhammad's proof, the summary of which is that the purpose of denial is to negate the will in the past and
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on whether a testator's denial (juhud) of having made a will constitutes revocation (ruju'). The issue arises from the matn of al-Quduri, who states that denial is not revocation, which Imam Muhammad agrees with. Imam Abu Yusuf argues that denial is revocation because revocation negates the will in the present, while denial negates it in both past and present, so it is a stronger negation. The commentary then notes a potential reconciliation: the divergent reports from Imam Muhammad (in al-Jami' al-Kabir, denial is not revocation; in al-Mabsut, it is) may be reconciled by context—if the denial is in the presence or absence of the legatee. However, the author of al-Hidayah and others hold that the statement in al-Jami' al-Kabir is Imam Muhammad's own view, and the one in al-Mabsut is Imam Abu Yusuf's, and this is correct. Thus the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that denial of the will does not constitute revocation. Imam Muhammad's proofs include: (1) denial negates the past, so non-existence in the present is automatic, making the denial meaningless if the will was real; (2) revocation affirms past existence and negates present, while denial negates both past and present, so it is not revocation in reality, just as denial of marriage is not divorce.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): Denial by the testator that he ever made a will.
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a will; the act of nullifying a bequest.
موصیٰ لہ (musa lahu): The legatee, the beneficiary of the will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The will becomes conditional upon the testator's later confirmation

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a testator's verbal denial of a will does not revoke it; the will remains valid unless an actual act of revocation occurs.

The Denial of a Will: Does It Constitute Revocation? — An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proofs and tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether denial of a will constitutes revocation, explain the proofs of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and identify the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Denial of a will does not revoke it — the realities of denial and revocation are fundamentally different.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on the effect of a testator's denial of having made a will. While both agree that revocation of a will is permissible, they differ on whether a simple denial amounts to revocation. Understanding their differing proofs and the resolution of this debate is essential for correctly applying the rules of wills in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine whether the testator's denial (juhud) of a will is legally considered a revocation (ruju'). Note that both Imams agree on the permissibility of revocation but disagree on the effect of denial.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that revocation negates the will in the present, while denial negates it in both past and present. Since denial includes a stronger negation, it should a fortiori be considered revocation.
A fortiori (awla) argument: if the lesser (present negation) is revocation, the greater (past+present negation) must also be.
3
Examine Imam Muhammad's first proof
Imam Muhammad argues that denial is false if a will was actually made, so it is futile. The negation in the present is merely a necessary consequence of the false past negation, not an intended revocation.
The testator's falsehood makes the denial legally ineffective.
4
Examine Imam Muhammad's second proof
The realities of revocation and denial are distinct: revocation affirms the past and negates the present, while denial negates both. Therefore, they cannot be equated. The analogy with marriage denial (which does not constitute divorce) supports this.
Qiyas: Denial of marriage (juhud al-nikah) does not dissolve the marriage, so denial of a will should not revoke it.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction and identify the mufta bihi
Imam Muhammad's statements in al-Jami' al-Kabir (denial not revocation) and in al-Mabsut (denial is revocation) are reconciled by attributing the Mabsut view to Abu Yusuf. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that denial is not revocation.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah prefers this interpretation.

Book of Wills. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya Volume 16. Whether the testator's denial of the will is considered revocation of the will or not, statements of jurists: He said: 'Whoever denies the will, it is not a revocation.' Thus mentioned Muhammad. And Abu Yusuf said: 'It is a revocation, because revocation is a negation in the present, while denial is a negation in the past and present, so it is more fitting that it be a revocation.' Translation: Al-Quduri said: Whoever denies the will, this denial will not be a revocation. Muhammad mentioned likewise. And Abu Yusuf said: It will be a revocation because revocation is negation in the present, and denial is negation in the past and present, a fortiori it will be a revocation. Explanation: Previously it was mentioned that revocation of a will is permissible and some actions that indicate revocation were mentioned. Now here this issue is being explained: if the testator denies the will itself, what is the ruling? The author presented two statements: one of Imam Muhammad and the other of Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad stated that this denial will not be a revocation. Imam Abu Yusuf said that this will be a revocation. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof: The reality of revocation is that it affirms the will in the past and negates it in the present. Denial of the will means negation in the past and also in the present. So if negation in the present constitutes revocation, then negation in both past and present a fortiori constitutes revocation. Warning: Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Kabir that denial is not a revocation, and in al-Mabsut he mentioned that denial is a revocation. Some jurists said that what is mentioned in al-Mabsut is applicable when the revocation is in the presence of the legatee, and what is in al-Jami' al-Kabir is applicable when the revocation is in the absence of the legatee. And some said that what is in al-Jami' al-Kabir is Imam Muhammad's own statement, while what is in al-Mabsut is the statement of Imam Abu Yusuf. The author of Hidayah is of this view, and it is correct. (Zaylai p. 718 v. 6). Two proofs of Imam Muhammad: 'And for Muhammad: Denial is a negation in the past, and the negation in the present is a necessary consequence of that. If it is established in the present, the denial becomes futile. Or because revocation is an affirmation in the past and a negation in the present, while denial is a negation in both past and present, so it is not a revocation in reality. For this reason, denial of marriage is not a separation.' Translation: And Muhammad's proof: Denial negates in the past, and its being negated in the present is a necessary result of that. And when the falsehood in the present is established, the denial becomes futile. And also because revocation is affirmation in the past and negation in the present, while denial is negation in both past and present, so it will not be a revocation in reality. For this reason, denial of marriage is not considered a divorce. Explanation: This is Imam Muhammad's proof. Its gist: The purpose of denial is to negate the will in the past. When the past is negated and no change has occurred, the present is also considered negated. The author says that the negation in the present is due to the requirement of negation in the past. Since in this case it is assumed that the testator has made a will but now denies it, it becomes clear that the testator is lying. When his falsehood is established, his denial does not actually negate the past; rather it is invalid. When the negation of the past is invalid, the negation of the present is a fortiori invalid, so the testator's denial becomes futile. Second proof of Imam Muhammad: The reality of revocation is different from that of denial. Revocation involves affirmation in the past and negation in the present. Denial involves negation in both. Since their realities are distinct, denial cannot constitute revocation in reality. Similarly, denial of marriage is not considered divorce or separation because their realities are distinct. Denial aims to negate marriage in the past, while divorce aims to affirm in the past and negate in the present. Just as these two cannot be used metaphorically in place of each other due to opposition, similarly denial and revocation are opposed, so neither can be used in place of the other.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the testator's denial of having made a will (juhud al-wasiyyah) constitutes a revocation (ruju'). The text presents two positions: Imam Muhammad holds that denial is not revocation, while Imam Abu Yusuf holds that it is. The commentary first explains Abu Yusuf's reasoning: revocation negates the will in the present while affirming it in the past; denial negates it in both past and present, so it should a fortiori be revocation. A tanbih (footnote) notes that Imam Muhammad's own statements appear contradictory: in al-Jami' al-Kabir he says it is not revocation, but in al-Mabsut he says it is. The commentator resolves this by mentioning that some jurists reconcile the two by applying each to different contexts (presence vs. absence of the legatee), while others attribute the Mabsut view to Abu Yusuf. The author of al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) prefers the latter interpretation. Then two proofs are given for Imam Muhammad. First: denial aims to negate the will in the past; the present negation is incidental. But since the testator's denial is false (he actually made the will), his past negation fails, so the entire denial is futile. Second: revocation and denial have different realities—revocation affirms the past and negates the present, while denial negates both. Therefore, denial cannot be revocation in reality, analogous to how denial of marriage (juhud al-nikah) does not constitute divorce (furqah). The commentary emphasizes that the Hanafi school follows the position that denial is not revocation, as per the relied-upon view of Imam Muhammad and the author of Hidayah.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): Denial of a will; the testator's statement that he never made such a will.
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a will by the testator; legal annulment of the will during his lifetime.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or will; a disposition of property taking effect after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, if a testator denies having made a will, what is the effect?
A) The will is automatically revoked.
B) The will remains valid because denial is not considered revocation.
C) The will is void because the testator lied.
D) The will is suspended until the testator affirms it.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a testator's denial of having made a will does not revoke the will; the will remains valid unless there is explicit or implied revocation.

Verbal Expressions That Revoke a Will
Explains which words by testator revoke a will and the proof that denial is not revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish revocatory expressions (batil, tark) from non-revocatory ones (haram, riba, takhir) and state the Hanafi proof that denial does not revoke a will.

Core Idea
Only words that directly cancel the will (batil, tark) effect revocation; describing the will as haram or riba affirms its existence.

When a testator makes a will, he retains the right to revoke it at any time. But not every word said about the will counts as revocation. The Hanafi school, drawing on the reasoning of Imam Muhammad, distinguishes between expressions that negate the will's future effect (revocation) and those that merely describe it. This lesson unpacks the exact words that constitute valid revocation and the rational basis for this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key question
What verbal expressions by the testator result in the revocation of a will? The passage answers for specific phrases: 'batil', 'tark', 'haram wa riba', and 'takhir'.
2
Analyze the proof that denial is not revocation
Imam Muhammad provides a rational proof: revocation (ruju') affirms the will's existence in the past and negates it in the present; denial (juhud) negates in both past and present. They are opposites, so denial cannot be revocation.
This is supported by the analogy that denying marriage is not considered divorce.
3
Examine the expression 'haram wa riba'
Describing a will as 'haram' or 'riba' presupposes the will exists. Such descriptions are attributes that require a subject; therefore they do not cancel the will.
This differs from saying 'batil' which directly asserts the will has ceased to exist.
4
Examine the expression 'takhir' (deferral)
Deferring a will does not cancel it, similar to deferring payment of a debt. The will remains valid but postponed.
In contrast, saying 'taraktu' (I left/abandoned) is an explicit cancellation.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
Only expressions that directly indicate extinction (batil) or abandonment (tark) constitute valid revocation. Other expressions merely describe or postpone the will, leaving it intact.
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

بخلاف اس صورت کے جب کہ موسی نے کہا ترکٹ اس لئے کہ ترک اسقاط ہے۔ تشریح اگر موصی نے وصیت کے بعد یہ الفاظ استعمال کئے کہ میری کی ہوئی ہر وصیت حرام اور سود ہے تو یہ رجوع نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ جب کوئی چیز کسی وصف سے متصف ہوتی ہے تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہوتا ہے کہ وہ چیز بذات خود موجود ہے لہذا اس کا حرام ہوتا اور سود ہونا وصیت کا وصف ہے تو جب اس کا وصف باقی ہے تو وصیت بدرجہ اولی باقی ہے اور جب وصیت باقی ہے تو اس کو رجوع قرار نہیں دیا جائے گا، البتہ اگر موصی نے یوں کہا ہو کہ میری کی ہوئی تمام وصیتیں باطل ہیں تو یہ وصیت سے رجوع ہو گا اس لئے کہ باطل اس چیز کو کہتے ہیں جو معدوم ہوگئی ہو اور ختم ہوگئی ہو اور معدوم ہو جانا دلیل رجوع ہے۔ اور اگر موصی نے یوں کہا کہ میں نے وصیت کو مئوخر کر دیا تو اس سے رجوع نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ تاخیر کا مقصد اسقاط نہیں ہے۔ جیسے مقروض کو اداء قرض میں مہلت دینا اس کا مقصد یہ نہیں ہے کہ قرض ہی ختم ہو گیا ہے لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ تاخیر سے اہتقاط نہ ہو گا، البتہ اگر موصی نے یوں کہا ہو کہ میں نے وصیت کو چھوڑ دیا تو یہ رجوع ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ ترک ( چھوڑ نا ) اسقاط ہی ہے لہذا رجوع ثابت ہوگا۔
When negation has been made in the past and no change is found, then in the present it is also considered subject to doubt. The author said: The negation in the present occurred due to the requirement of the need for negation in the past. Since in this issue it is assumed that the testator had made a will, but now he denies it, so it becomes known that the testator is lying. And when his lying is established, due to his denial, negation in the past is not proven, rather it is invalid. And when the negation in the past became invalid, the negation in the present is even more so invalid. Therefore, the testator's denial is futile. The second proof of Imam Muhammad is that the reality of revocation (ruju') is different and the reality of denial (juhud) is different. In revocation, there is affirmation in the past and negation in the present. In denial, there is negation in both places. So when the realities of the two are different, how can denial be revocation in reality? This is the reason why denying marriage is not considered separation or divorce because the realities of the two are different. The purpose of denying marriage is to negate marriage in the past, and the purpose of divorce is affirmation in the past and negation in the present. Just as these two, due to opposition, are not used as metaphors for each other, similarly there is opposition between denial and revocation; therefore, neither can be used in place of the other. If the testator says after the will: 'Every will of mine is haram and riba', these words do not constitute revocation. Similarly, if the testator says: 'I have deferred the will', that also is not revocation. And if he says: 'Every will I made for so-and-so is haram and riba', it is not revocation because the description necessitates the continuation of the original, unlike if he said: 'It is batil' because batil is that which is gone and extinct. And if he said: 'I deferred it', it is not revocation because deferral is not for cancellation, like deferring a debt, unlike if he said: 'I left it' because leaving is cancellation. Translation: And if the testator said: 'Every will that I made for so-and-so is haram and riba', this is not revocation because the description requires the continuation of the original, unlike the case when he said: 'It is batil' because batil is that which is finished and extinct. And if he said: 'I deferred the will', it is not revocation because deferral is not for cancellation, like deferring a debt, unlike the case when he said: 'I left it' because leaving is cancellation. Explanation: If the testator used these words after making a will: 'Every will I made is haram and riba', this is not revocation because when something is described with a quality, it means that thing exists in itself. So describing it as haram and riba is a description of the will; when its description remains, then the will remains a fortiori. And when the will remains, this is not considered revocation. However, if the testator said: 'All my wills are batil', this is revocation because batil is that which has become extinct and finished, and being extinct is evidence of revocation. And if the testator said: 'I have deferred the will', this is not revocation because the purpose of deferral is not cancellation. Like giving a debtor respite in paying the debt, its purpose is not that the debt has ended; so it is known that deferral does not cancel. However, if the testator said: 'I have left the will', this is revocation because leaving (tark) is cancellation; therefore, revocation is established.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah/Ashraf al-Bidayah explaining the rulings related to revocation of a will (ruju' al-wasiyya). The author first addresses the issue that a testator's denial (juhud) of having made a will does not constitute revocation. The proof by Imam Muhammad is presented: denial negates the will in both past and present, while revocation affirms the will in the past and negates it only in the present. Since their realities are opposite, denial cannot be a form of revocation. The analogy of marriage denial not being divorce is cited to illustrate this point. Then, the passage examines specific verbal expressions: if the testator says 'my will is haram and riba,' this does not revoke the will because describing something as haram or riba presupposes its existence. Such expressions are merely attributes of the will, not cancellation. In contrast, if he says 'my will is batil,' this is a revocation because batil means it has ceased to exist. Similarly, saying 'I have deferred the will' is not revocation because deferral implies the will still exists but is postponed, like deferring payment of a debt does not cancel the debt. However, saying 'I have left the will' (taraktu) does constitute revocation because leaving implies abandonment and cancellation. The Hanafi school’s relied-upon position is that only words that directly indicate cancellation (batil, tark) effect a valid revocation.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyya): A bequest of property made by the testator to take effect after his death.
رجوع (ruju'): The act of revoking a will or contract by the testator.
جحود (juhud): Denial of having made a will; it is a lie (kizb) if the will was actually made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator says: 'My will is haram and riba.' What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) This is a valid revocation of the will.
B) This does not revoke the will because the description implies the will still exists.
C) This revokes only half of the will.
D) This is considered a new will.

Key Takeaway
Only explicit cancellation words (batil, tark) revoke a will; describing it as haram, riba, or deferring it does not.

Revocation by Explicit Wording vs Second Will in Bequests
Rules when testator says a previously bequeathed slave belongs to someone else

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between revocation and partnership when a testator names a new beneficiary for a bequeathed slave, and apply the Hanafi rulings on the validity of such statements.

Core Idea
Saying 'huwa li-fulan' revokes a bequest; making a second will creates equal partnership, not revocation.

In Hanafi inheritance law, a testator may change his mind about a bequest. This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that the wording matters: an explicit statement 'this slave is for so-and-so' revokes the earlier bequest, while simply naming another beneficiary in a second will does not revoke but results in joint ownership. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of bequests (wasiyya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the testator’s statement type
Determine whether the testator used explicit wording like 'huwa li-fulan' or simply made a second will. The exact phrasing is crucial for the legal effect.
The phrase 'huwa li-fulan' indicates exclusive ownership; 'awsaytu bihi li-akhar' does not.
2
Apply the rule for explicit revocation
If the statement is 'huwa li-fulan', it is a valid revocation. The first bequest is cancelled, and the new beneficiary takes the property solely.
Revocation requires clear wording that severs the prior bequest.
3
Determine effect when a second will is used
A second will without revocation language creates a partnership: the property is shared equally between the two beneficiaries because the subject matter can be divided and the word 'wasiyya' is suitable for partnership.
Contrast with explicit revocation which does not allow partnership.
4
Consider death scenarios
If the second beneficiary named in the revocation statement was dead at the time, the first bequest remains. If the second beneficiary dies after the statement but before the testator, both bequests are void and the property goes to the testator's heirs.
The revocation takes effect only if the second beneficiary is alive at the time of the statement.

The testator said: 'The slave I bequeathed to Khalid is for Zaid' – this is also evidence of revocation. Similarly, if he said: 'This slave belongs to such-and-such an heir', this is also a revocation of the first. And if he said: 'The slave I bequeathed to so-and-so is for so-and-so', it is a revocation because the wording indicates cutting off partnership (shirka), unlike if he bequeathed it to one person then bequeathed it to another, because the object can accommodate partnership and the wording is suitable for it. Similarly, if he said: 'It is for my heir so-and-so', it is a revocation of the first for the reason we have stated, and it becomes a bequest for the heir, whose ruling we have mentioned. If that other person (the second) was dead at the time of the statement, the first bequest remains as it was because the first bequest is only invalidated by necessity of being for the second, but since that was not realized, it remains for the first. If that person was alive when he said that, then died before the testator's death, then the bequest goes to the testator's heirs due to the invalidity of both bequests – the first by revocation, the second by death. And Allah knows best. Translation: And if the testator said: 'That slave which I bequeathed to so-and-so is for so-and-so', this is a revocation because the word indicates cutting off partnership, unlike the case where he bequeathed the slave to one person then bequeathed it to another, because the object can be shared and the wording is suitable for sharing. Similarly, if he said: 'He is for my heir so-and-so', this is a revocation of the first for the reason we have stated, and it becomes a bequest for the heir, and we have mentioned its ruling. If that second so-and-so was dead at the time of the bequest, the first bequest remains as it was because the first bequest is only invalidated by necessity of being for the second, but that was not realized, so it remains for the first. If that so-and-so was alive when he said that, then died before the testator's death, then the bequest goes to the testator's heirs due to the invalidity of both bequests – the first by revocation, the second by death. And Allah knows best. Explanation: If the testator first bequeathed a slave to Khalid, and then the testator said: 'I make the bequest I made to Khalid for Zaid', this is clearly a revocation, and the testator's intention is to remove it from Khalid and bequeath it to Zaid. Question: Could it be considered shared between Khalid and Zaid? Answer: There is no word that can establish partnership; rather, the testator's speech clearly proves that there is no partnership, but that the slave is specifically for Zaid. And if he first bequeathed this slave to Khalid and then made a second bequest of the same to Zaid, then here the slave will be divided half and half between the first and second because in this speech there is no word used that indicates cutting off partnership, and the word 'wasiyya' (bequest) is suitable for partnership.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah deals with cases where the testator, after bequeathing a slave to one person, makes a statement indicating that the slave belongs to another. The rule is that if he says 'the slave I bequeathed to X is for Y' (using the word 'huwa' – he is), this constitutes a clear revocation of the first bequest because the wording severs the connection (partnership). However, if the testator simply makes a second will (awsaytu bihi li-akhar) for the same slave to another person, this does not revoke the first; instead, the slave is shared equally between the two beneficiaries because the subject matter can be divided and the word 'wasiyya' allows partnership. The passage also discusses two additional scenarios: (1) If the second beneficiary named in the revocation statement was dead at the time of the statement, then the first bequest remains valid because the revocation was conditional on the second being alive, which was not the case. (2) If the second beneficiary was alive at the time of the statement but died before the testator, then both bequests become void – the first due to revocation, the second due to the beneficiary's death – and the slave goes to the testator's heirs as part of the estate. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a bequest by the testator's statement that the bequeathed property belongs to another.
وصية (wasiyya): A bequest made by the testator to take effect after death.
شَرِكة (shirka): Partnership; here referring to joint ownership of the bequeathed slave between two beneficiaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator first bequeaths a slave to Khalid. Later, he says: 'The slave I bequeathed to Khalid is for Zaid.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave belongs to Khalid and Zaid equally.
B) The slave goes to Zaid only, and the bequest to Khalid is revoked.
C) The slave goes to Khalid only, as the second statement is invalid.
D) The slave goes to the testator's heirs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, explicit wording of exclusive ownership (huwa li-fulan) revokes a previous bequest, while a mere second will results in equal partnership between the beneficiaries.

Revocation of Will by Designating for an Heir
Ruling on revocation of will via new will for an heir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a testator's declaration to change a will in favor of an heir constitutes valid revocation, and apply the Hanafi ruling to scenarios involving the timing and validity of the second will.

Core Idea
A will is revoked only if the new designation for an heir is valid at the time of declaration.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies how a testator's declaration regarding a will for an heir can revoke a prior bequest. The ruling hinges on the validity of the second will at the moment of utterance. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the law of wasiyyah, particularly when the testator attempts to redirect a bequest to an heir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the declarative act
The testator declares that a previously bequeathed property is now a will for his heir. This declaration is the act under analysis.
The act is a verbal or written statement of conversion.
2
Determine the condition for valid revocation
Revocation of the first will occurs only if the second will (for the heir) is valid at the time of the declaration. Validity here means the second legatee is alive and capable of inheriting.
The second will must be legally sound for revocation to take effect.
3
Analyze Scenario 1: Second legatee deceased at declaration
If at the moment of the testator's declaration, the heir (second legatee) is already dead, the second will is invalid. Consequently, the first will remains valid and effective.
No revocation because the second will is void ab initio.
4
Analyze Scenario 2: Second legatee alive at declaration but dies before testator
If the second legatee was alive when the testator spoke but dies before the testator, then both wills become invalid. The first will is revoked by the declaration, and the second will fails due to the legatee's predecease.
Property returns to the testator's estate to be distributed among all heirs.
5
Apply the ruling to variations
Generalize: the first will's validity depends on the second will's initial validity. Use these two scenarios as a framework for similar cases.
The key variable is the second legatee's status at the time of declaration.

Source Text

كتاب الوصايا
شرکت کا احتمال رکھتا ہے یعنی وصیت میں شرکت کی صلاحیت ہے۔
اور اگر موصی نے یوں کہا ہو کہ میں نے فلاں کو جو وصیت کر رکھی ہے پس وہ سامان میرے فلاں وارث کے لئے وصیت ہے تو یہ بھی رجوع ہو جائے گا یعنی موصی نے پہلی وصیت سے رجوع کر لیا اور اب اس کو وارث کے لئے وصیت قرار دیا جائے گا اور وارث کے لئے وصیت کا کیا حکم ہے اس کا تفصیلی بیان ماقبل میں گزر چکا ہے۔ لیکن جب موصی نے دوسرے موضی لہ کو وصیت کی اس وقت موضی لۂ ثانی زندہ نہیں ہے بلکہ وصیت سے پہلے ہی مر چکا ہے تو پہلی وصیت باطل نہ ہوگی بلکہ وصیت برقرار رہے گی اس لئے کہ پہلی وصیت صرف اس وقت ٹوٹتی ہے جب کہ وصیت دوسرے کے لئے صحیح ہو اور کے لئے ہوتی صیح اور درست جائے اور یہاں وصیت دوسرے کے لئے صحیح نہیں ہوتی تو پہلی وصیت صحیح اور درست ہے۔ اور اگر موجی کے یہ کہنے کے وقت موضعی لۂ ثانی زندہ ہے لیکن وہ موصی کی موت سے پہلے ہی مر گیا تو اب وصیت دونوں کے حق میں باطل ہے اور یہ مال وصیت موصی کے ورثہ کا ہوگا۔ پہلی وصیت کے باطل ہونے کی وجہ موصی کا اس سے رجوع ہے اور وصیت ثانیہ کے باطل ہونے کی وجہ موصی لۂ ثانی کا موصی کی موت سے پہلے مر جانا ہے واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah, commentary on Hidayah Urdu, volume 16, page 114. Kitab al-Wasaya. It contains the possibility of partnership, i.e., there is capacity for partnership in a will. And if the testator said: 'I have made a will for so-and-so, so that property is a will for my heir so-and-so,' then this also constitutes revocation, i.e., the testator has revoked the first will, and now it will be considered a will for the heir. The detailed ruling of a will for an heir has already passed earlier. But when the testator made the will for the second legatee, if at that time the second legatee was not alive but had died before the will, then the first will is not invalidated; rather the will remains effective. Because the first will is only broken when the will for the second person is valid and correct. And here the will for the second person is not valid, so the first will is valid and correct. And if at the time of the testator's saying it, the second legatee was alive but he died before the testator's death, then now the will is invalid with respect to both, and this property of the will belongs to the testator's heirs. The reason for the first will being invalid is the testator's revocation of it, and the reason for the second will being invalid is the second legatee's death before the testator's death. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kitab al-Wasaya. It discusses the revocation of a will when the testator declares that the previously bequeathed property is now a will for an heir. The ruling is that such a declaration constitutes a valid revocation. However, the revocation is contingent on the second will being valid at the time of utterance. If the second legatee was already dead at that time, the first will remains intact. If the second legatee was alive at the time of the declaration but dies before the testator, then both wills become invalid, and the property returns to the testator's estate. This is because the first will is revoked by the second, and the second fails due to the legatee's predecease. The commentary follows the order of the original text, explaining each scenario clearly.

Key Terms
وصیت (wasiyyah): A bequest or will by which a person directs disposition of his property after death.
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation of a will by the testator through words or actions indicating withdrawal.
موصی لہ (musa lahu): The legatee, i.e., the person in whose favor the will is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a testator says 'My will for Zaid is now for my heir Amr,' but at that moment Amr is already dead, what happens to the original will for Zaid?
A) It is revoked because the testator intended revocation.
B) It remains valid because the second will is invalid.
C) Both wills are invalid.
D) The property goes to the testator's heirs.

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a will by designating an heir occurs only if the second will is valid at the time of declaration; if the second legatee dies later, both wills fail.

The Central Role of the One-Third Limit in Wills
Preface explaining the focus on one-third of property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the one-third limit is the central principle governing issues in Kitab al-Wasaya.

Core Idea
The one-third limit is the axis around which all will rulings turn.

This lesson introduces the chapter on wills concerning one-third of property. The text explains that the entire chapter revolves around the one-third limit. Understanding this will help you navigate the specific rulings that follow, as the one-third rule balances the testator's freedom with the heirs' rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
Read the passage and note the phrase 'محور ثلث مال' (axis is one-third of property). This tells you that the one-third limit is not just one topic but the central organizing principle.
2
Understand the significance of 'axis'
Reflect on why the one-third limit is given such prominence. It is the default maximum for a will without heir consent, so many rules depend on it.
The limit protects heirs while allowing some testamentary freedom.
3
Connect to broader fiqh
Consider how this chapter fits into the larger structure of Kitab al-Wasaya. The preliminaries cover general principles, then the chapter on one-third delves into the detailed applications.
All issues related to multiple wills, revocation, etc., are discussed with respect to the one-third limit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بابُ الوصيّة بثلث المال
ترجمہ یہ باب ہے مال کے ثالث کی وصیت کرنے کا
تشریح کتاب الوصایا کے مسائل عامہ کا محور ثلث مال ہے اس لئے کتاب الوصایا کے مقدمات کو بیان کرنے کے بعد ان مسائل کو بیان کیا گیا جو اس باب سے تعلق رکھتے ہیں۔
Chapter of will concerning one-third of property. Translation: This chapter is about making a will of one-third of property. Explanation: The axis of the general issues of Kitab al-Wasaya is the one-third of property, therefore after mentioning the preliminaries of Kitab al-Wasaya, those issues that pertain to this chapter are mentioned.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This is front matter introducing the chapter 'Bab al-Wasiyyah bi Thulth al-Mal'. It explains that the central theme of Kitab al-Wasaya is the one-third of the estate, so after the introductory discussions, the specific rulings related to this chapter are presented. This orients the student to the importance of the one-third rule in Islamic inheritance law.

Key Terms
ثلث المال (thulth al-mal): One-third of the testator's property, the maximum limit for a will without heirs' consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main topic of Bab al-Wasiyyah bi Thulth al-Mal?
A) Wills that are exactly one-third of the estate.
B) All will issues where the one-third rule applies.
C) Wills for multiple legatees.
D) Revocation of wills.

Key Takeaway
The one-third limit is the central axis of Kitab al-Wasaya; all will-related rulings revolve around it.

Equal Division of One-Third Between Two Legatees
Ruling when two separate legatees each get one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two separate legatees each receive a one-third will and explain why they share the one-third equally.

Core Idea
Two equal wills to different persons share the one-third equally when heirs refuse.

A testator may will up to one-third of his property without heir consent. But what happens if he wills one-third to two different people? The total requested exceeds the allowable one-third. This lesson explains how the law resolves the conflict between equal claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
The testator made two separate wills: one-third to each of two persons. The combined demand is two-thirds, but only one-third is available without heir approval.
Assume heirs do not permit the excess.
2
Apply the one-third limit
Without heir consent, the will cannot exceed one-third of the estate. Therefore, only one-third is available for distribution.
This rule is established in previous lessons.
3
Determine the basis of entitlement
Both legatees have the same cause of entitlement (sabab al-istihqaq): the will itself. Their claims are equal in strength and origin.
Neither has priority by time or amount.
4
Apply equal division
Since the claims are equal and the one-third can be shared, the available one-third is divided equally between the two legatees.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اوصى لرجل بثلث ماله ولاخر بثلث ماله ولم تجز الورثة فالثلث بينهما لانه يضيق الثلث عن حقهما اذ لا تزاد عليه عند عدم الاجازة على ما تقدم وقد تساويا فى سبب الاستحقاق فيستويان في الاستحقاق والمحل يقبل الشركه فيكون بينهما
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے کسی شخص کے لئے اپنے تیسرے مال کی وصیت کی اور دوسرے کے لئے اپنے تیسرے مال کی اور ورثا نے اجازت نہ دی تو ثلث ان دونوں کے درمیان ہوگا اس لئے کہ ثلث ان دونوں کے حق سے تنگ ہے اسلئے کہ ورثا کی اجازت نہ ہونے کے وقت وصیت ثلث سے بڑھائی نہیں جا سکتی اس تفصیل کے مطابق جو مقدم ہو چکی ہے اور یہ دونوں استحقاق کے سبب میں مساوی ہیں تو استحقاق کے اندر دونوں مساوی ہوں گے اور محل شرکت کو قبول کرتا ہے تو ثلث ان دونوں کے درمیان ہوگا۔ تشریح ماقبل میں تفصیلات گزر چکی ہیں کہ ایک تہائی سے زیادہ مال کی اگر وصیت کی تو ورثہ کی اجازت سے اس کا نفاذ ہو سکتا ہے ورنہ نہیں اب دیکھئے کہ زید نے اپنے تیسرے مال کی وصیت خالد کے لئے کر دی اور بکر کے لئے بھی ثلث مال کی وصیت کر دی تو ان دونوں وصیتوں کا مال ثلثان ہو گیا اور محل نفاذ وصیت فقط ثلث ہے اور ورثا نے ثلث سے زیادہ میں اجازت نہیں دی ایک تہائی خالد اور بکر کے در میان آدھا آدھا ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ ایک ثلث میں اتنی وسعت و گنجائش تو ہے نہیں کہ اس میں سے ہر ایک کو پورا پورا ثلث مل جائے کیونکہ جب ورثہ کی اجازت نہ ہو تو وصیت ثلث پر زیادہ نہیں کی جاسکتی اور سبب استحقاق میں دونوں برابر ومساوی ہیں تو پھر استحقاق کے اندر بھی دونوں مساوی ہوں گے اور تیسرے مال کے اندر بھی یہ صلاحیت ہے کہ اس میں شرکت ہو سکتی ہے لہذا ثلث کو ان دونوں کے درمیان مشترک قرار دیا جائے گا۔
He (al-Quduri) said: Whoever makes a will for a man of one-third of his property and for another of one-third of his property, and the heirs do not permit, then the one-third is between them (equally) because the one-third is insufficient for both rights, since it cannot be increased beyond one-third when there is no permission, as previously mentioned. And they are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they are equal in entitlement, and the subject accepts partnership, so it is between them. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And whoever made a will for one person of his one-third property and for another of his one-third property, and the heirs did not permit, then the third is (divided) between them both because the third is insufficient for both rights, since when there is no permission from heirs, the will cannot be increased beyond one-third, according to the detail that has preceded. And these two are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they are equal in entitlement, and the subject accepts partnership, so the third will be between them. Explanation: Details have preceded that if a will is made for more than one-third, execution is possible with heirs' permission; otherwise not. Now see: Zaid made a will of one-third of his property for Khalid and also made a will of one-third for Bakr. So the total of the two wills becomes two-thirds, and the subject of execution of the will is only one-third. The heirs did not permit more than one-third. So the one-third will be divided equally between Khalid and Bakr. Because the one-third does not have such capacity and room that each of them can get a full one-third, since when there is no permission of heirs, the will cannot be increased beyond one-third. And they are equal in the cause of entitlement, so they are equal in entitlement. And the one-third also has the capacity that partnership can take place in it. Therefore, the one-third will be declared common (to be divided equally) between them.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Quduri, expanded by the commentator. The case: a testator wills one-third of his property to two different persons (each gets one-third, total two-thirds). The heirs do not permit the excess. The ruling: the one-third is divided equally between the two legatees. The reason: the one-third is insufficient for both full shares, and since both have equal entitlement (same cause: will), they share equally. The property (one-third) can be subject to partnership. This illustrates the Hanafi rule that without heir consent, the will cannot exceed one-third, and if multiple wills compete, they share proportionally based on their respective shares as designated.

Key Terms
ثلث (thulth): One-third of the testator's property.
سبب الاستحقاق (sabab al-istihqaq): Cause of entitlement, i.e., the will itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a testator wills one-third to A and one-third to B, but the heirs only permit one-third, how is the one-third divided?
A) Half to A, half to B.
B) One-third to A, two-thirds to B.
C) The entire one-third goes to the first named.
D) The will is invalid.

Key Takeaway
When two people are each left one-third of a testator's property and only one-third is available, they share that one-third equally because their cause of entitlement is identical.

Proportional Reduction of Wills When Total Exceeds One-Third
Ruling on proportional division of competing wills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of proportional division when multiple wills compete within the one-third limit, using the analogy of creditors splitting an insolvent estate.

Core Idea
When wills exceed one-third, legatees divide the available third proportionally to their designated shares.

A testator may will different fractions to multiple people. If the total exceeds one-third and heirs do not permit, only the one-third is distributed. How is it divided? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling: the one-third is split proportionally based on the designated shares, not equally. The logic mirrors how creditors divide an insolvent estate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The testator wills one-third to one person and one-sixth to another. Total desired is one-half, exceeding the one-third limit without heirs' permission.
The wills are valid but limited to one-third of the estate.
2
Recognize the insufficiency
Only one-third is available; it cannot satisfy both full shares. So the one-third must be divided.
Heirs do not permit, so the excess fraction is not given.
3
Determine proportionality
The one-third is divided in the ratio of the designated shares: one-third (two parts) to one-sixth (one part), i.e., 2:1.
Thus the one-third is split into three shares: two for the larger, one for the smaller.
4
Apply the analogy of creditors
The ruling is analogous to creditors (as-hab al-dayn) dividing an insolvent estate proportionally. Both cases involve valid rights and a limited pool.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the illa of insufficient funds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اوصى لاحدهما بالثلث ولاخر بالسدس فالثلث بينهما اثلاثا لان كل واحد منهما يدلى بسبب صحيح وضاق الثلث عن حقيهما فيقسمانه على قدر حقيهما كما فى اصحاب الديوان فيجعل الاقل سهما والاكثر صار ثلثة اسهم ب الاقل وسهـمان لصاحب الأكثر
ترجمہ اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک کے لئے ثلث کی اور دوسرے کے لئے سدس کی وصیت کی تو ثلث ان دونوں کے درمیان تین حصوں پر تقسیم ہو گا اس لئے کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک سبب صحیح کی وجہ سے استحقاق رکھتا ہے اور ثلث ان دونوں کے حق سے تنگ ہو گیا تو وہ ثلث کو اپنے حق کے بقدر تقسیم کریں گے جیسے اصحاب الدیون میں تو اقل کو ایک سہم اور اکثر کو دو سہم قرار دیا جائے گا تو تین سہم ہو گئے ایک سہم صاحب اقل کیلئے اور دو سہم صاحب اکثر کے لئے۔
تشریح اگر موصی نے خالد کے لئے ثلث مال کی وصیت کی اور بکر کے لئے سدس کی جس کا کل مال نصف ہو گیا اور نصف بغیر ورثا کی اجازت ان کو نہیں دیا جا سکتا تو صرف ثلث ہی ان دونوں کو دیا جائے گا جس کی صورت یہ ہوگی کہ خالد کو ثلث میں سے دو حصے اور بکر کو ایک حصہ ملے گا کیونکہ دونوں کا استحقاق وصیت کے ذریعہ سے ہوا ہے اور یہ سبب صحیح ہے اور ثلث میں اتنی وسعت نہیں ہے جو ان دونوں کے حق کو پورا کر سکے لہذا جیسے اصحاب دیوان ترکہ کو اپنے اپنے حق کے تائب سے تقسیم کرتے ہیں اسی طرح یہ دونوں موصی لہ بھی کریں گے ( اصحاب الدیون کی تفصیل ہم درس سراجی میں بیان کر چکے ہیں) لہذا ان کے حق میں یہی تناسب ہے کہ خالد کا حق دو حصے ہے اور بکر کا ایک حصہ لہذا ثلث کو بھی تین حصوں پر تقسیم کر کے اس تناسب سے تقسیم کر دیا جائے گا کہ خالد کے لئے دو حصے ہوں گے اور بکر کے لئے ایک حصہ ہوگا۔
And if he made a will for one of them of one-third and for the other of one-sixth, then the one-third is between them in thirds (i.e., divided into three shares) because each of them presents a valid cause, and the one-third is insufficient for both their rights, so they divide it according to the measure of their rights, as in the case of creditors of the estate. So the lesser is made one share and the greater two shares, becoming three shares: one share for the owner of the lesser, and two shares for the owner of the greater. Translation: And if he made a will for one of them of one-third and for the other of one-sixth, then the one-third is divided among them into three shares, because each of them has entitlement due to a valid cause, and the one-third is insufficient for both rights, so they divide it according to the extent of their rights, like in the case of creditors of the estate. So the lesser is made one share and the greater two shares, thus three shares: one share for the owner of the lesser and two shares for the owner of the greater. Explanation: If the testator made a will of one-third of property for Khalid and of one-sixth for Bakr, the total becomes one-half. Without heirs' permission, only the one-third can be given. So Khalid gets two parts of the one-third and Bakr gets one part. Because both have entitlement through the will, a valid cause. The one-third cannot satisfy both full rights, so they divide proportionally, like creditors of the estate divide the estate proportionally. So the ratio of their rights is 2:1 (since one-third is double one-sixth), so of the one-third, Khalid gets two-thirds of that one-third, i.e., two-ninths of the whole, and Bakr gets one-third of the one-third, i.e., one-ninth. The text simplifies by dividing the one-third into three shares: two for Khalid and one for Bakr.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This ruling statement, from Quduri's matn with commentary, addresses the scenario where a testator wills one-third to one person and one-sixth to another. The total is one-half, exceeding the one-third limit. Since heirs do not permit, only the one-third is distributed. The division is proportional to the designated shares: the one-third is divided into three parts, with two parts (for the one-third) to the first legatee and one part (for the one-sixth) to the second. The reasoning is analogous to how creditors divide an insolvent estate proportionally. This shows the Hanafi method of proportional reduction when multiple wills compete within the one-third limit.

Key Terms
ثلث (thuluth): One-third of the testator's estate, the maximum that can be willed without heirs' consent.
سدس (suds): One-sixth of the testator's property.
اصحاب الدیون (as-hab al-dayn): Creditors of the deceased who have debts against the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator wills 1/3 to X and 1/6 to Y. Heirs permit only 1/3. What fractions of the whole estate do X and Y receive?
A) X: 1/6, Y: 1/6
B) X: 2/9, Y: 1/9
C) X: 1/3, Y: 0
D) X: 1/9, Y: 2/9

Key Takeaway
When multiple wills compete within the one-third limit, the available third is divided proportionally according to the designated shares, akin to creditor distribution.

8 min
Dividing a Bequest That Exceeds One-Third: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on division of one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when multiple bequests exceed the one-third limit, and apply their proportional method to calculate legatee shares.

Core Idea
When bequests exceed one-third, division among legatees is proportional (Sahibayn) or equal (Abu Hanifa) — a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the distribution of the permitted one-third when a testator bequeaths all his property to one legatee and one-third to another. The heirs do not consent, so only one-third is executable. The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, lies in whether to divide that one-third equally or proportionally. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the principles of wasiyyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bequests
The testator wills all his property to one person (A) and one-third to another (B). The heirs do not permit, so only one-third of the estate is available for distribution among legatees.
The one-third limit applies because the heirs' consent is not given.
2
Determine the available portion
Since the total bequests (100% + 33.33%) exceed one-third, only one-third of the estate is given to the legatees.
This is the standard rule of wasiyyah in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's proportional method
The Sahibayn hold that the bequests should be treated proportionally: A's bequest (all) is three times B's (one-third), so the available one-third is divided into 1+3=4 shares. A gets 3/4 of the one-third, B gets 1/4.
Thus, A receives 1/4 of the total estate, B receives 1/12.
4
Apply Abu Hanifa's equal method
Abu Hanifa holds that both legatees have equal right to the one-third, so it is divided equally: each gets half of the one-third, i.e., 1/6 of the total estate each.
This is based on the idea that the cause (sabab) of their entitlement is the same.
5
Note the exceptions
Abu Hanifa does not give the legatee of the whole more than one-third except in specific cases: muhabah (undervalued transaction), sa'ayah (earnings), and darahim mursalah (loose dirhams).
These exceptions are not elaborated in the text but indicate boundaries of his rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اوصى لاحدهما بجميع ماله ولاخر بثلث ماله ولم تجز الورثة فالثلث بينهما على اربعة اسهم عندهما وقال ابو حنيفة الثلث بينهما نصفان ولا يضرب ابو حنيفة للموصى له ما زاد على الثلث الا في المحاباة والسعاية والدراهم المرسلة
ترجمہ اور اگر موصی نے ان دونوں میں سے ایک کے لئے اپنے پورے مال کی وصیت کی اور دوسرے کے لئے اپنے تیسرے مال کی اور ورثا نے اس کی اجازت نہیں دی تو صاحبین کے نزدیک ثلث ان دونوں کے درمیان چار حصوں پر ہوگا، اور ابوحنیفہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ ثلث ان دونوں کے درمیان نصفین ہوگا اور ابوحنیفہ موصی لہ کو ثلث سے زیادہ کا حصہ نہیں دیتے سوائے محاباۃ، سعایۃ اور دراہم مرسلہ کے۔
And if he made a will for one of them of all his property and for the other of one-third of his property, and the heirs did not permit, then the one-third is between them into four shares according to them (the Sahibayn). And Abu Hanifa said: The one-third is between them half-half. And Abu Hanifa does not give the legatee more than one-third except in the cases of al-muhabah, al-sa'ayah, and al-darahim al-mursalah. Translation: And if the testator made a will for one of them of all his property and for the other of one-third of his property, and the heirs did not permit, then according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the one-third is divided between them into four shares. And Abu Hanifa said that the one-third is half-half between them. And Abu Hanifa does not give the legatee what exceeds one-third except in the cases of muhibbah (gift), sa'ayah (earning), and mursala dirhams (loose dirhams).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The case: a testator wills all his property to one person and one-third to another. Without heirs' permission, only one-third can be given. According to the Sahibayn, that one-third is divided into four shares: the legatee of the entire property is deemed to have a share of three (as his designated share is 1, but effectively he claims the whole, so his 'right' is considered three times the other's?), and the other legatee has one share, so the one-third is divided 3:1. Abu Hanifa, however, says the division is equal (half-half) because both have entitlement through a will, and the one-third should be shared equally. The commentary notes Abu Hanifa's rule that a legatee cannot be given more than one-third (the fraction itself) except in specific contexts (muhabah, sa'ayah, etc.). The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is typically the view of the Sahibayn in later Hanafi practice, as it aligns with proportional reasoning.

Key Terms
صاحبین (sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main students of Abu Hanifa.
محاباة (muhabah): A transaction undervaluing property, sometimes treated like a gift in certain contexts.
سعایة (sa'ayah): Earnings; possibly a specific case where a slave is allowed to earn and bequest is involved.
دراہم مرسلہ (darahim mursalah): Loose dirhams; possibly a specific type of bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, how is the one-third divided when one legatee is willed all property and another is willed one-third?
A) Equally between them.
B) Three quarters to the first, one quarter to the second.
C) Two thirds to the first, one third to the second.
D) The entire one-third goes to the first legatee.

Key Takeaway
The distribution of bequests exceeding the one-third limit is not necessarily equal; intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf provides two valid methods, with the Sahibayn's proportional method being the mufta bihi.

Bequest of a Specific Item from a Nonexistent Category is Void
States the ruling voiding a bequest of a specific item from a nonexistent category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a bequest of a specific item from a nonexistent category and distinguish it from a bequest expressed as part of general wealth.

Core Idea
A bequest of a specific item from a nonexistent category is void; from wealth it is valid as value.

Wording in a bequest matters greatly. The testator's choice of words determines whether the bequest is for a specific physical item (ayn) or for value. This lesson examines the case where a testator bequeaths 'a goat from my goats' but owns no goats. The Hanafi ruling provides a clear principle that branches into many sub-cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and wording
Recognize that the testator said 'a goat from my goats' – this specifies a particular category.
The phrase 'from my goats' indicates the testator intends a specific goat from his flock.
2
Check the condition of existence
Determine whether the testator actually had goats at the time of the will or at death.
In this case, he had no goats.
3
State the ruling
The bequest is void (batil) because the specified item does not exist.
The ruling is based on the fact that the testator's intention was for a specific 'ayn.
4
Contrast with alternative wording
If he had said 'a goat from my wealth', the bequest would be valid as a bequest of value.
The phrase 'from my wealth' indicates intention for value, not a specific animal.
5
Extract the general principle
The principle: when a bequest is tied to a specific category that does not exist, it is void; when tied to the testator's general wealth, it is valid for value.
Many sub-cases branch out from this rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو قال شاة من غنمى ولا غنم له فالوصية باطلة لانه لما اضافه الى الغنم علمنا ان مراده عين الشاة حيث جعلها جزء من الغنم بخلاف ما اذا اضافها الى المال وعلى هذا يخرج كثير من المسائل
Or if he says: 'a goat from my goats' and he has no goats, then the bequest is void, because when he added it to the goats we know that his intention is the specific goat as he made it a part of the goats, unlike if he added it to the wealth. And upon this many issues branch out.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This is a ruling statement concerning the validity of a bequest when the testator specifies a particular animal from a category he does not possess. The key distinction is between specifying an item as part of a particular category (like 'from my goats') versus specifying it as part of his general wealth (like 'from my wealth'). In the first case, the bequest is for the specific animal, which is nonexistent at the time of the will and at the time of death, so it is void. In the second case, the bequest is for the value (maliat), which is valid. This ruling is from the Hanafi school and serves as a general principle from which many sub-cases branch out.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyya): Bequest; a testamentary disposition of property to take effect after death.
شاة (shah): A specific goat (or sheep).
عين ('ayn): A specific physical entity (as opposed to value or debt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid because intention is the value
B) Void because the specific animal does not exist
C) Valid only if the testator had goats at the time of the will
D) Void only if the testator explicitly says 'my goats'

Key Takeaway
A bequest of a specific item from a nonexistent category is void; the same item expressed as part of general wealth is valid for its value.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dividing a Bequest to Ummahat Awlad, Fuqara, and Masakin
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interpreting definite plurals in bequests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad in dividing a bequest to multiple categories, and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
When a bequest uses definite plurals, Shaykhayn treat each category as one recipient; Imam Muhammad requires two per category.

This passage deals with a specific inheritance scenario: a testator bequeaths one-third of his wealth to three ummahat awlad (slave mothers of his children), to the fuqara, and to the masakin. The question is how to divide the shares among these three categories. The two leading authorities of the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaykhayn), disagree with Imam Muhammad on the interpretation of the plural nouns 'fuqara' and 'masakin' when preceded by the definite article. The dispute hinges on the meaning of the definite plural: does it indicate the genus (jins) and thus the minimum of one, or does it indicate multiplicity, requiring at least two? The passage resolves the issue by following the Shaykhayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the scenario
The dispute is between Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad. The scenario: a bequest of one-third to three specific ummahat awlad, and to two general categories: fuqara and masakin.
The ummahat awlad are counted as three individuals, but fuqara and masakin are indefinite categories.
2
State the positions and the numerical division
Shaykhayn: divide into 5 shares (3 for ummahat, 1 for fuqara, 1 for masakin). Imam Muhammad: divide into 7 shares (3 for ummahat, 2 for fuqara, 2 for masakin).
The total shares differ because of the different treatment of the plural.
3
Extract the proofs for each position
Imam Muhammad argues that the plural noun 'fuqara' and 'masakin' indicate at least two, citing inheritance rules (Quranic usage). Shaykhayn argue that the definite article 'al-' indicates the genus, which can be satisfied by a single individual, especially when distribution to all is impossible (ta'azzur al-sarf).
The proof involves linguistic usul on the meaning of al-jam' al-muhalla.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference in share count directly affects the distribution. Under Shaykhayn, each category gets less shares (1 each) compared to Muhammad's (2 each).
The mufta bihi is the position of Shaykhayn as stated by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من اوصى بثلث ماله لا مهات أولاده وهن ثلث وللفقراء والمساكين فلهن ثلثة اسهم من خمسة اسهم قال الله عنه وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف وعن محمد انه يقسم على سبعة اسهم لهن ثلثة ولكل فريق رضی سهمان واصله ان الوصية لامهات الاولاد جائزة والفقراء والمساكين جنسان وفسرنا هما في الزكوة. لمحمد ان المذكور لفظ الجمع وادناه في الميراث اثنان نجد ذالک فی القرآن فكان من كل فريق اثنان وامهات الاولاد ثلث فلهذا يقسم على سبعة ولهما ان الجمع المحلى بالالف واللام يراد به الجنس وانه يتناول الادنى مع احتمال الكل لاسيما عند تعذر صرفه الى الكل فيعتبر من كل فريق واحد فبلغ الحساب خمسة والثلثة للثلث
He said: And whoever bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his ummahat awlad (mothers of his children) and they are three, and to the fuqara and masakin, then they have three shares out of five shares. The author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And from Muhammad: that it is divided into seven shares: for them three shares, and for each faction two shares. The basis is that bequest to ummahat awlad is permissible, and fuqara and masakin are two categories, and we explained them in Zakat. For Muhammad: that the mentioned word is plural, and the minimum in inheritance is two, as we find in the Quran, so from each faction two, and ummahat awlad are three, therefore divided into seven. For them (Shaykhayn): that the plural with the definite article is for the genus, and it includes the minimum with the possibility of all, especially when it is difficult to refer to all, so one from each faction is considered, so the count reaches five, and three shares for the three.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding the division of a bequest to three categories: three ummahat awlad, fuqara, and masakin. The issue is the interpretation of the plural nouns 'fuqara' and 'masakin' when preceded by the definite article 'al-'. Imam Muhammad argues that the minimum meaning of the plural in inheritance contexts is two (based on the Quranic verse 'فان كان له اخوة' where 'اخوة' implies at least two), so each category should receive two shares, making seven total. The Shaykhayn hold that the definite article indicates the genus (jins), which encompasses the singular minimum, especially when distribution to all individuals is impossible (ta'azzur al-sarf). Thus each category is considered as one recipient, giving five shares. The text clearly states that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Shaykhayn, as it is introduced as 'the author said... this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf'. Also note the important principle that bequest to ummahat awlad is permissible by istihsan despite the qiyas prohibiting it because they are still slaves (milk al-yamin). The categories of fuqara and masakin are distinct, as explained in the book of Zakat.

Key Terms
الجمع المحلى بالالف واللام (al-jam' al-muhalla bi al-alif wa al-lam): A plural noun preceded by the definite article, interpreted by Shaykhayn as indicating the genus (jins) and encompassing the singular.
ادنى الجمع (adna al-jam'): The minimum number of individuals implied by a plural form; in inheritance law, Imam Muhammad holds it is two; Shaykhayn hold it is one (when definite).
أمهات الأولاد (ummahat al-awlad): Female slaves who have borne a child to their master; they become free upon their master's death and can be beneficiaries of a bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two shares each
B) One share each
C) Three shares each
D) One share total

Key Takeaway
When a bequest uses the definite plural for categories, the Hanafi school (Shaykhayn) treats each category as one recipient, unless individuals are specified.
